General Description[edit]

Generations of archaeologists have been amazed that the geographical location of Cahokia had "almost no inhabitants' until 1000 CE.[6] Suddenly there was an influx of people of more than one group[7] - the "population surges by at least an order of magnitude within decades" [8] - bringing with them to this heretofore vacant spot a new social and settlement structure and an obsession with moundbuilding.[9] Bill Romaine (2009) has noted, based on lunar alignments used at Cahokia, there were cultural similarities to a Toltec site in Arkansas.[10]

The period of 1000-1150 CE is thus one of great change and demographic expansion. The previous settlement pattern of nucleated clusters of houses "was abandoned in favor of widely scattered single-family farmsteads"[11] between which were structures "with special ritual and social significance."[12] Whilst most Cahokians were self-sufficient granaries were also present.[13] There were likely at least 50,000 people supported within the the 2000 Km2 region of 'greater Cahokia'[14] of which about 15,000 lived in an area called the 'central administrative complex'.[15]

Many archaeologists are skeptical whether there was a ruler king at Cahokia[16] the polity more likely led by a "a priesthood or a group of ruler-priests"[17] within a social strata that included included chiefs, sub-chiefs, elders, priests, and other religious functionaries."[18] The Cahokians were capable of feats of organization that included the famous Monks Mound and other mounds which required moving 1.1 million m3 or earth and a 15m high wooden palisade that ran for nearly 3km.[19]

Social Scale[edit]

♠ Polity territory ♣ [2000-3000] ♥ in squared kilometers

"Eric Rupley, however, calculated the catchment needed to feed 15,000 people would be 625 square kilometers, which is well within the possible land area available in the American Bottom."[20]

"The Cahokia heartland is about 2000 to 3000 square kilometers" [21]


♠ Polity Population ♣ [40,000-50,000] ♥ People.

40,000-50,000 is a widely agreed upon number

Milner estimates the American Bottom population ("population figures for Cahokia were doubled to approximate the inhabitants of all mound centers and added to valley-wide estimates for small settlements") in the Moorehead phase had fallen about 25% from the Stirling total. [22] Which was:

"It is likely that at least 50,000 people lived within the 2000 square kilometer “greater Cahokia” region at its height (ca. A.D. 1100)." [23]
"George Milner estimates that there were roughly 8000 people in the Cahokia central administrative complex and up to 50,000 in the greater Cahokia region after AD 1050. Before that the neither had large populations—perhaps less than 1000 people in the entire greater Cahokia region." However: "With new excavations at East St. Louis the estimate for the central administrative complex needs to be increased to something like 15,000."[24]


♠ Population of the largest settlement ♣ [10,000-15,000] ♥ Inhabitants. Cahokia.

10,000-15,000 is a widely agreed upon number

Milner estimates that by the Morehead phase the Cahokia (i.e. city) population had fallen about 40% from the Lohmann peak, [25] which was:

"At its height (ca. A.D. 1100) the central administrative complex at Cahokia contained at least 15,000 residents though this high population was very short lived (probably less than 100 years)."[26]
"“central administrative complex” (CAG) and was roughly 14 square kilometers in

area."[27]

"“greater Cahokia” a region extending outward from Cahokia roughly 30 kilometers"[28]


Hierarchical Complexity[edit]

♠ Settlement hierarchy ♣ [4-5] ♥ levels.

4 levels widely-agreed upon.

"There was a four-tier hierarchy of sites, and these sites were integrated in terms of the organization of ritual space and in terms of material culture traits."[29]
"There were at least four in terms of settlement, but it is not clear how or even if they were “stacked” organizationally. Smaller mound centers may have been independent but not “separate” from the larger Cahokian polity, perhaps reflecting a form of “complicated factionalism” in a network of elite controlling families."[30]


5 level alternative hypothesied as [31]:

1. Hamlets (family head, 4 or 5 houses. 5-10 km away. On agricultural soils. Some argue seasonal. Could also be permanent.)
2. Hamlet integration site (single mound site)
3. Inner village ritual site
4. Multi-mound centers
5. Cahokia


Settlement

1. "First is the central administrative complex, containing three large mound and plaza complexes (Cahokia, East St.Louis, and St. Louis)."[32]
2. "The second is an area of regular interaction which might be called “greater Cahokia.” This area contains 8-12 mound a plaza complexes with their own internal divisions."[33]
3. "Third is a larger regional scale, including, for example, sites of the Langford Tradition and the Spoon River Focus. We do not have a good understanding of how sites in the larger region tie together. Each region seems to be separated by buffer zones. Perhaps each region reflects a single polity."[34]
4. "During most of the Mississippian phases, communities outside of Cahokia were small and moundless, referred to as homesteads, farmsteads or hamlets. However, a number of villages of small to moderate size were scattered throughout the area, some with one or two mounds, which were probably local centers with special functions." [35]

Cahokia 120 mounds; East St. Louis 45 mounds; St. Louis 26 mounds.[36]

♠ Administrative levels ♣ [3-4] ♥ levels.


1. King ?

Hypothesised level. Between 1050-1150 CE there may have been a king. However, a majority of scholars may disagree.


1. Chief / Priest

"Members of the highest social strata probably included chiefs, sub-chiefs, elders, priests, and other religious functionaries." [37]
"Cahokia may have been led by a priesthood or a group of ruler-priests, but a shift to “king” does not appear to have happened at Cahokia."[38]
"The central administrative complex represents the core of the Cahokian polity. The location of ridgetop mounds within this area may equate with kin groupings or other administrative units. East St. Louis, being newer, may have been a higher status community of isolated elites."[39]
At Mound 72 "Analysis of the skeletal remains shows that certain burial groups were of higher status than others and that some may have come from places other than Cahokia." [40] New analysis of the skeletons in the burial suggest they were of a man and a woman. "'Now we realize we don’t have a system in which males are these dominant figures and females are playing bit parts,' Emerson said. He explained that this interpretation of the beaded burial is more in line with what is known about the fertility and agricultural symbolism found in the rest of the ancient city."[41]


2. Sub-chief / Sub-priest?

"Members of the highest social strata probably included chiefs, sub-chiefs, elders, priests, and other religious functionaries." [42]
"The answers provided by the working group seem to point to Cahokia being an urban settlement that was the center of a regional government, but the picture is not entirely clear." [43]
"Regional political integration appears to have been an essentially ritual one; that is, the site hierarchy that is present appears to be more of a hierarchy of ritual spaces than of political jurisdictions."[44]
"Cahokia was also the center of a regional government of some kind, at least for a short period of time."[45]
"mound complexes may have been organized around sodalities rather than around kin groups. Perhaps these sodalities were secret societies"[46]
"Mound and plaza groups may represent corporate (perhaps kin-based) political and

ritual complexes, each of which would have been maintained by their own administrative specialists or generalized leader."[47]


3. Elder / Religious functionary

Professions[edit]

♠ Occupational complexity ♣ ♥ "Cahokia does not appear to have achieved the organizational complexity expected through the scaling processes described by Ortman ... There is little evidence for occupational specialization and virtually no evidence for diversification of occupations." [67]

Traders.[68]

"The case for Mississippian period craft specialization rests on the existence of well-made objects, the great numbers of some particularly attractive items, and the contexts of the tools and debris related to making them."[69]

arrowheads
represents the "strongest case for specilization based on artifact appearance ... The people who made the Mound 72 points were pushing the limits of what can be accomplished by chipping stone. ... "the length-to-width ratios of the American Bottom points were more uniform than those of the points from distant places." However "High-quality local chert ... is much easier to knap than the coarse-grained Hixton silicified sandstone. In fact, the uniformity of points made with the latter stone indicates that these knappers were able to work a difficult material with great precision." On this and other evidence Milner concludes "there was little, if any, difference among the most skilled knappers in the midcontinent in terms of their ability to produce uniformly shaped arrowheads for special events."[70]
figurines and figure-pipes
certain pottery vessels e.g. Ramey Incised jars
marine shell beads (which have identified tools: "small chert microdrills and sandstone saws").
It is argued that these beads "were among the artifacts that fueled Cahokia-dominated exchange systems that spanned the midcontinent.[71] However "The great majority of beads possessed by even the highest ranked people were of indifferent quality. These beads were identical to those held in far smaller numbers by common people. Only the individuals who held the most exalted positions ... had substantial numbers of especially attractive beads ... at least some of which were part of matched sets of uniformly shaped beads." Milner concludes "there is no compelling reason to believe that bead making was conducted in anything other than domestic settings." [72]

Milner notes that "Similarly impressive objects were made in many small-scale societies throughout the world that lacked specialists in the sense of the term used by those who see a highly elaborated economy in the American Bottom."[73]

♠ Professional military officers ♣ ♥ Iconography may indicate professional officers or soldiers of some kind. People that look like they are wielding weapons with head scalps attached to belts. Depictions of people who look like they are going to and from battle.

♠ Professional soldiers ♣ ♥ Iconography may indicate professional officers or soldiers of some kind. People that look like they are wielding weapons with head scalps attached to belts. Depictions of people who look like they are going to and from battle.

♠ Professional priesthood ♣ present ♥ "Those who planned and organized the construction of the Cahokian cosmographical landscape can be interpreted as being religious specialists."[74]

Bureaucracy characteristics[edit]

♠ Full-time bureaucrats ♣ inferred absent ♥ There is no written record for Cahokia[75] so there probably were no full-time administrators in a formal state bureauracy. If administrators did exist they may have used a non-written method of record-keeping (e.g. perhaps something similar to knots in string known from early Cuzco) but there is no evidence for this in the archaeological record. Activity within the "central administrative complex"[76] may have largely been of religious significance and perhaps communal or elite storage rather than used as sites explicitly for the administration or processing of taxes and management of records (for which we have no evidence). One might hypothesise that the new "social roles linked to community defense, organization of labor, and communal storage of maize in secure central places"[77] of the preceding Emergent Mississippian were developed into something more formal and institutional in this period but this does not necessarily mean a state bureaucracy with full-time officials responsible to a king. Whatever institutions were in place at Cahokia to manage resources, they did not require a centralized institution for record-keeping with an extensive hierarchy of full-time officials.

♠ Source of support ♣ ♥

"fields for the chiefs worked part-time by commoners, or through tribute."[78]

♠ Examination system ♣ absent ♥

♠ Merit promotion ♣ ♥

♠ Specialized government buildings ♣ inferred present ♥ "East St. Louis started out as a residential group, but evolved into an administrative/storage like complex." [79] The identification of any Mississippian-period structures in the Cahokia region as specialized government buildings is far from clear, but the sites of activity within the 'central administrative complex'[80] may have combined administrative and ceremonial functions. Peter Peregrine discussed the evidence in an email to us: 'There are a few larger buildings that could have been "meeting rooms", but were those ceremonial or administrative, or just big houses (they have fire pits)? Was there a difference? There were also the henges and secondary plazas. I guess I would argue that there are multi-use administrative "places" throughout the Cahokia, but maybe not formal buildings as such.'[81]

Specialized Buildings: polity owned[edit]

♠ Communal buildings ♣ present ♥ "Interspersed among the single-family farmsteads were a few structures with special ritual and social significance. They included sweatlodges ... and a large structure that probably had some community significance." [83]

♠ Special purpose house ♣ present ♥

♠ Utilitarian public buildings ♣ present ♥ Massive public architecture [84] Public works undertaken by commoners.[85]

♠ irrigation systems ♣ absent ♥ "There is evidence of maize pollen in swales, and some drainage and irrigation facilities." [86] However, there were "no irrigation systems evident at Cahokia."[87]
♠ drinking water supply systems ♣ absent ♥
♠ markets ♣ absent ♥ There is no evidence for markets, "nothing that would suggest an integrated economy of any kind."[88] "There were probably no markets at Cahokia. Distribution of food and manufactured goods (e.g. shell beads) were likely “event based”, taking place at feasts and rituals. Barter or reciprocal exchange was likely part of an informal economy that circulated goods on a limited basis. Some redistribution of surplus production may have taken place as well." [89]
♠ food storage sites ♣ present ♥ "Most of the people at Cahokia were self-sufficient, but granaries are present in Stirling/Moorehead Cahokia."[90] "Fluctuation in agricultural production (especially due to flooding) would have affected specific areas of the American Bottom on an almost annual basis, and may have required provisioning some parts of the population on an irregular basis. Granaries and other storage facilities may have held the surplus required for this provisioning."[91]

♠ Symbolic buildings ♣ present ♥

"Cahokian architecture is distinct, and has a religious flavor in terms of marker posts and Lshaped buildings. But there appear to be no burials of paramount chiefs at Cahokia."[92]

Woodhenge. [93]

The beaded blanket burial has been interpreted as a paramount chief but some doubt this and there is still confusion over the matter. [94]

Shrines. [95]

"A large building was present on top of Monks Mound and atop other mounds at Cahokia, but how they were used is unknown."[96]

Cemeteries both for high rank and low rank. [97]

Grand Plaza [98] which as artificially created. [99]

Mounds (3 types) [100]

Ridgetop
Conical
Platform

Milner calculates that the total volume of earth moved to create all the mounds at Cahokia (those currently present and those that existed before they were destroyed) was 1,177,701 cubic meters, of which 621,921 cubic meters was deposited at Monks Mound. [101] Averages to 5889 m3 per year over 200 year period, which is 16.13 m3 per day, or in modern terms 161 wheelbarrow deliveries (100 litre capacity). "Cahokia's mounds did not necessitate inordinate amounts of work, a large population, or both. The heaviest likely labor demand - about 500 workers spending 10 days each year on moundbuilding during the two earliest Mississippian phases - could have been accommodated by 2,500 people." [102]

Stone structure within Monk's Mound 12 m below surface of second terrace. "All we know is that it goes at least 32 feet in one direction (northwest-southeast); we don't know how thick it is or how wide an area it covers. We know it does not occur under the whole mound, as no vertical cores have ever encountered stone. ... This stone mass is probably lime stone, and the closest sources would be sixteen to twenty-four kilometers (ten to fifteen miles) from Cahokia, so it had to be used for something special." [103]


♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ present ♥

Games on Grand Plaza. 15 chunkey stones found in Mound 72. [104]


♠ Knowledge/information buildings ♣ absent ♥

Transport infrastructure[edit]

♠ Roads ♣ present ♥ [105] "Roadways link external centers to Cahokia providing a physical connection between them."[106] "LiDAR helped to identify a causeway 25m wide from Monks Mound to Rattlesnake Mound." [107] "trail networks also are important, and some of the historic east-west ones cross near Cahokia."[108] "Emerald, for example, was out on the prairie, and may have been a pilgrimage site, as roadways connected it to Cahokia and to the Southeast."[109]
♠ Bridges ♣ absent ♥ There were no bridges in prehistoric North America.
♠ Canals ♣ absent ♥
♠ Ports ♣ absent ♥ "There was geographically widespread trade between Cahokia and other communities (and between those other communities themselves) especially along the Mississippi. However, this trade appears to have been low volume, with only small amounts being exchanged at any given time. Canoes identified so far are small, unable to carry high volumes of commodities. There is no evidence for centralized control of this exchange, except perhaps for high-status goods and exceptional ritual objects." [110]"[111]

The most impressive/costly building(s)[edit]

♠ height ♣ ♥ in m
♠ extent ♣ ♥ in m (the horizontal dimension along the longest axis)
♠ cost ♣ ♥ in people-years. Monks Mound built in 20 years. "Workers are thought to have loaded baskets at a nearby source of fill dirt, called a borrow pit, and then shaped the dirt and clay into a flat-topped pyramid 30 meters [100 feet] tall — all without wheels, metal tools, or beasts of burden." "Parts of it appear to have been built from whole blocks of sod, rather than basketfuls of soil."[112] Volume about 22 million cubic feet or 621,921 cubic meters. [113] Height: 30 meters. [114] 86.37 cubic meters (30.5 tons) per day over 20 years.

Monks Mound

Monks Mound. Area: 5.6 ha (at 128m contour line). Height: 30 meters. [115] Volume about 22 million cubic feet or 621,921 cubic meters. [116] Construction began "sometime during the late Emergent Mississippian" (900-1000 CE) "and the mound basis reached its final form by the Stirling phase." [117] (Stirling phase: 1050-1150 CE). "In a study of Sonoran Indians, Erasmus found that one man using a digging stick excavated 2.6 m3 of soil in five hours (1965). For Cahokia calculations, Erasmus's figures for two carriers were averaged to obtain 1.675 m3 and 0.838 m3 moved in fiver hours over 100 m and 200 m, respectively. Productivity dropped after five hours, so this workday length was retained in the estimates provided here. The American Bottom excavators had to work with clays that were more difficult to dig than sandy Sonoran soils; however their stone hoes were more efficient than mere sticks. ...The distance over which soil was carried has a major effect of the Cahokia labor estimates. Certainly much of the mound fill was derived from borrow pits that dotted the site, several of which were quite large (Fowler 1989). In addition, an unknown, but certainly large, fraction of mound fill consisted of upper soil horizons that had been scraped from broad areas. ... In general, it seems appropriate to use a distance of 100 m when calculating the labor needed to construct 102 of the mounds. A distance of 200 m was used for Monks Mound, following Reed and coworkers (1968). ... Using these figures, the mounds could have been built in 470,000, 490,000, 155,000, and 86,000 five hour days, distributed across the Lohmann, Stirling, Moorehead, and Sand Prairie phases." (Figures have been rounded). [118] Construction "could have been accomplished by 470, 490, 310, and 68 laborers who worked 10 five-hour days each year during the Lohmann, Stirling, Moorehead and Sand Prairie phases, using phase durations of 100, 100, 50, and 125 years." [119]

Palisade 2.8km in length, 15m in height according to Iseminger et al. [120] Whilst the mounds were easily built over hundreds of years by a small number of workers, working few hours in a year, "partial walls were useless" and so arguably amounted to the more impressive challenge.[121] In terms of time and resources the first palisade was the biggest challenge because subsequent palisades could initially incorporate what was left standing from the earlier one.[122] Conservative estimate, 291,000 hours spent building each palisade. "1,000 workers could have erected a formidable wall in two to three months" [123] "If Cahokia's residents could afford to move more slowly, taking nine months to complete the job, then 220 to 340 laborers were needed." [124]

Sites not associated with residential areas.

♠ Ceremonial site ♣ present ♥

♠ Mines or quarries ♣ present ♥ "Large chert cores were roughed out at quarries, not at valley sites." [127] From earliest times people of American bottom were visiting a number of sources. This is not mentioned in current literature. Two examples: Wyandot, in the Ohio river valley and Mill Creek just south of the American bottom.

Measurement System[edit]

♠ Length ♣ present ♥ "There is no evidence for standards of weights or volumes (although there may have been a standard unit of length used to lay out ritual spaces)."[128]
♠ Area ♣ absent ♥
♠ Volume ♣ absent ♥
♠ Weight ♣ absent ♥
♠ Time ♣ present ♥ "The Toltec site in Arkansas is an example of a major center with lunar alignments. Bill Romaine (2009) argues that this esoteric understanding of the moon/lunar standstill 18.6 year cycle was transferred from Toltec to Cahokia about AD 1050. Emerald, Illinois, seems to have similar alignments ca. AD 1000-1050 along with lunar shrines with roadways.[129] Woodhenge: "From the center post, the sun priest could observe the rising sun on the eastern horizon as it lined up with certain perimeter posts. This calendar could be used to determine the winter and summer solstices and spring and fall equinoxes, as well as other important dates in the ceremonial calendar. There were at least five Woodhenges built at Cahokia in the same general location." [130]
♠ Geometrical ♣ absent ♥
♠ Other ♣ ♥

♠ Other thrown weapons ♣ Spears ♥ Projectile point sizes indicate no usage of spears after about 1200 CE. [134] Not to be confused with atlatl darts. Fortifications[edit]

♠ Settlements in a defensive position ♣ absent ♥ There were "fortified enclaves of Cahokians to the north, for example, at Aztalan in southeastern Wisconsin." [146] Settlements primarily located for access to water and arable land. [147]
♠ Wooden palisades ♣ present ♥ "The main mound and plaza region of Cahokia was palisaded after ca. A.D. 1200, also indicating a high level of violence."[148] "After about A.D. 1100 there is an increase in numbers of palisaded sites (they were present earlier at Toltec)." [149] The center of Cahokia was palisaded "late in the 1100s." This wall was rebuilt at least four times. [150] "Ceramic data and radiocarbon dates indicate that construction of all four stockades occurred during the Late Stirling and Moorehead phases and most likely over the one-hundred-year period from about AD 1175 to 1275." [151]
♠ Earth ramparts ♣ absent ♥
♠ Ditch ♣ absent ♥ Some suggestions of ditches at a couple of sites, but they were not common and were not present at Cahokia other than as borrow pits for levelling the plaza and building mounds. [152]
♠ Moat ♣ absent ♥
♠ Stone walls (non-mortared) ♣ absent ♥ Palisade 2.8km in length, 15m in height according to Iseminger et al. [153] Whilst the mounds were easily built over hundreds of years by a small number of workers, working few hours in a year, "partial walls were useless" and so arguably amounted to the more impressive challenge.[154] In terms of time and resources the first palisade was the biggest challenge because subsequent palisades could initially incorporate what was left standing from the earlier one.[155] Conservative estimate, 291,000 hours spent building each palisade. "1,000 workers could have erected a formidable wall in two to three months" [156] "If Cahokia's residents could afford to move more slowly, taking nine months to complete the job, then 220 to 340 laborers were needed." [157]
♠ Stone walls (mortared) ♣ absent ♥
♠ Fortified camps ♣ absent ♥ There were "fortified enclaves of Cahokians to the north, for example, at Aztalan in southeastern Wisconsin." [158] However, they were not camps, probably large villages of 500-1000 people. [159]
♠ Complex fortifications ♣ absent ♥
♠ Long walls ♣ 0 ♥ km.
♠ Modern fortifications ♣ absent ♥

General Characteristics of Warfare[edit]

Types of Warfare[edit]

♠ Inter-polity wars ♣ present ♥

There are mass burials of genetically distinct individuals in the “ridge” mounds, esp. Mound 72.

"Although there is no consensus on this point, some archaeologists have found evidence for warfare around Cahokia: burials with weapons, burned buildings, sacrificial victims, bodies found with embedded arrowheads or smashed skulls. The most convincing evidence is the erection of the four successive high defensive stockades or palisades at Cahokia, each with evenly spaced bastions for archers."[160]

♠ Internal wars ♣ suspected unknown ♥ The construction of the palisade suggests conflict was present. Since it surrounds only the central elite / ritual precinct, one interpretation was that it protected them rather than the community as a whole.

♠ Intra-elite conflicts ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Popular uprisings ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Military revolts ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Separatist rebellions ♣ suspected unknown ♥

"Elsewhere, I have argued that the production of a transregional peace in the early Mississippian world - the Pax Cahokiana - stemmed from the spread of a political-religious movement centered at the proto-urban ceter of Cahokia".[165]


Archaeological proxies:

♠ Skeletal evidence of violence ♣ present ♥ "The archaeological record for this area suggests that significant evidence of human skeletal trauma and fortification construction does not appear until after about AD 800-1000. Analysis of Late Woodland skeletal populations provides evidence that the recently introduced bow and arrow had been incorporated into local war practices."[166]

"The osteological evidence of intergroup violence in the northern midcontinent has been documented by many researchers - from across the region between AD 900 and 1400 are reports of violently crushed skulls, arrow points embedded in human bones, decapitation, and scalped heads."[167]

♠ Mass burials ♣ present ♥ There are mass burials of genetically distinct individuals in the “ridge” mounds, esp. Mound 72.[168]

♠ Settlement destruction ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No data yet found in the literature.

♠ Pictorial evidence of warfare ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No data yet found in the literature.

List of Wars[edit]

Ritual variables[edit]

A collective ritual is a ritual in which two or more people participate. With the official cult we refer to the set of collective ritual practices that are most closely associated with legitimation of the power structure (including elites, if any).

♠ RA ♣ Enrico Cioni ♥

♠ Expert ♣ ♥

Rites of passage

Funerary rituals: Mass burial at Mound 72: elite personage and sacrificed retainers.

Gatherings/ritual space

Cahokia has large rectangular plazas that would have accommodated thousands of visitors, as well as woodhenges and large mounds that were re-built and repaired regularly, probably on the occasion of large collective rituals--the assumption being that construction and repair were themselves important rituals. Evidence for chunkey ball game (cosmologically significant game, it also included high-stakes gambling in historical times).

Feasting

A lot of evidence for annual feasts at the site of Cahokia itself, probably between summer and early autumn. Large amounts of food consumed (including rare or special food like deer, swan, and fruit), many stimulants used (e.g. tobacco, caffeine-rich "black drink"). Thousands of guests likely, mostly commoners judging by vessels used/discarded.

Art

Long-nosed masks suggest early versions of ethnographically recorded "adoption" rituals meant to create kinship ties between unrelated groups.
Ramey Incised vessels engraved with symbols (mostly geometrical) that have been interpreted as reinforcing socio-political bonds between commoners and elites, and also expressing notions of fertility and life-force to do with agricultural ceremonies.
Magico-ritual items including worked bone.

Other

Caffeine-rich "black drink".
Human sacrifice occurred.

Sources

Brown, J.A. 1997. The Archaeology of Ancient Religion in the Eastern Woodlands. Annual Review of Anthropology 26: 465-495.
Crown,P.L., T.E. Emerson, J.Gu, W.J. Hurst, T.R. Pauketat and T. Ward. 2012. Ritual Black Drink consumption at Cahokia. PNAS 109(35): 13944-13949.
Pauketat, T.R., L.S. Kelly, G.J. Fritz, N.H. Lopinot, S. Elias and E. Hargrave. 2002. The Residues of Feasting and Public Ritual at Early Cahokia. American Antiquity 67(2): 257-279.

Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ summer/autumn feast ♥ "All of the lines of submound evidence point in the direction of a series of short-term and large-scale events involving feasts, the use of magicoritual and sumptuary goods, the shaping of craft goods and wood, and possibly the rethatching or reconstruction of architecture. The abundant insect remains clearly indicate that these events occurred during warm weather months. Fruits in the pit indicate both early summer (strawberries and mulberries), late summer (blackberries, elderberries, and grapes), and fall (persimmon) harvests, although all could have been eaten from dried stores. The deer and waterfowl may have been most easily captured in the early autumn (see Bent 1962) and certainly abundant starchy-seed crops would have been harvested by that time. Thus, a mid-summer to early autumn origin, at least for zones D2 and G, is most likely." [169] "It is worthy of mention that prominent individuals, such as those buried in Cahokia's Lohmann phase Mound 72 (Fowler 1991, 1997; Fowler et al. 2000), may have played key roles in Cahokia's commensal politics. However, most people taking part in such events would not have been of high rank or of esteemed reputation. So, regardless of who hosted or coordinated the presumed Grand Plaza rites, the events themselves would have been collective experiences that defy pigeonholing into status categories. Such events would have been simultaneously low status and high status or communal and political (see Pauketat 2000; Pauketat and Emerson 1999)." [170] "[T]he sub-Mound 51 pit dates to the earliest phase of the youngest and largest Mississippian polity in North America and may encapsulate the process whereby people accepted or accommodated (or even resisted) a Cahokian organization, identity, or way of life. That process seems to have involved a dramatically enlarged and centralized sense of community, polity, and economy. [...] That coordinated practices may have been components of a collective cultural process may be central to explaining how people accommodated the social and demographic shifts though to have attended the Lohmann phase regime in the Greater Cahokia region (see Pauketat 1998b)." [171]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ "All of the lines of submound evidence point in the direction of a series of short-term and large-scale events involving feasts, the use of magicoritual and sumptuary goods, the shaping of craft goods and wood, and possibly the rethatching or reconstruction of architecture. The abundant insect remains clearly indicate that these events occurred during warm weather months. Fruits in the pit indicate both early summer (strawberries and mulberries), late summer (blackberries, elderberries, and grapes), and fall (persimmon) harvests, although all could have been eaten from dried stores. The deer and waterfowl may have been most easily captured in the early autumn (see Bent 1962) and certainly abundant starchy-seed crops would have been harvested by that time. Thus, a mid-summer to early autumn origin, at least for zones D2 and G, is most likely." [172]

♠ Duration ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ [2,400-20,900] ♥ This estimate is based on the number of ceramic vessels found in a series of discrete feasting contexts at Cahokia--specifically, 20,961 vessels were found in Zone D, 5,501 in Zone D1, 7.782 in Zone D2, 474 in Zone E (which in many ways is an anomalous context, and has therefore been excluded from my estimate), 2,466 in Zone F, 18,685 in Zone G, and 12,615 in Zone H [175]. If we consider that each of these vessels may have been used by a single participant, then the number of vessels should be the equivalent of the number of participants. Of course, each participant may have used a number of different vessels, so perhaps it is more sensible to halve the estimate. Expert guidance needed.

♠ entertainment ♣ present ♥ "Also a prominent component of such gatherings would have been the playing of a traditional sport, similar to the game known as “chunkey” in historic times (Culin 1992). The chunkey game involved opposing teams alternately rolling a finely polished disk-shaped stone across a level play- ing field (courtyard or plaza) and hurling gaming sticks after it to score points." [185]

Costs of participation
♠ currency ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No indication of this in the archaeological, iconographic or ethnohistorical record.

♠ human sacrifice of an out-group member ♣ inferred absent ♥ Human remains found in association with feasting residue at Cahokia are more likely to have been magicoritual items than the result of human sacrifice performed at the feasts themselves: "Cut marks were observed on a left fibula (both anterior and posterior to the midshaft) and on a right radius (inferior to the tuberosity on the proximal radius) suggestive of postmortem processing. [...] Carnivore gnawing in the form of tooth-grove furrows and tooth punctures was present on theproximal end of a left femur, the proximal end of a right ulna, and a right radius. [...] [T]he polish on a right femur was probably not a natural, postdepositional effect of sand abrasion, and the femur fragment may have been used as a tool. This fact, along with the cut marks and the evidence for the carnivore gnawing, indicates that these particular elements had been retained, used, and exposed for an indeterminate period of tie, perhaps in a mortuary facility such as a charnel house or temple (see DePratter 1991)." [191]

This fact, along with the cut marks and the evidence for the carnivore gnawing, indicates that these particular elements had been retained, used, and exposed for an indeterminate period of tie, perhaps in a mortuary facility such as a charnel house or temple (see DePratter 1991)." [192]
♠ human sacrifice of a relative ♣ inferred absent ♥ Human remains found in association with feasting residue at Cahokia are more likely to have been magicoritual items than the result of human sacrifice performed at the feasts themselves: "Cut marks were observed on a left fibula (both anterior and posterior to the midshaft) and on a right radius (inferior to the tuberosity on the proximal radius) suggestive of postmortem processing. [...] Carnivore gnawing in the form of tooth-grove furrows and tooth punctures was present on theproximal end of a left femur, the proximal end of a right ulna, and a right radius. [...] [T]he polish on a right femur was probably not a natural, postdepositional effect of sand abrasion, and the femur fragment may have been used as a tool. This fact, along with the cut marks and the evidence for the carnivore gnawing, indicates that these particular elements had been retained, used, and exposed for an indeterminate period of tie, perhaps in a mortuary facility such as a charnel house or temple (see DePratter 1991)." [193]

Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ purification ritual ♥ "[B]ased on ethnographic evidence and the presence of “sweat houses” on many Mississippian sites and earlier and later sites), ritual purification was probably the most common. This would have involved fasting, sweats, and perhaps taking purgatives (e.g. the “Black Drink”, but also perhaps plugs of tobacco)." [194]

♠ poisoning/dysphoria-causing drugs ♣ present ♥ "This would have involved fasting, sweats, and perhaps taking purgatives (e.g. the “Black Drink”, but also perhaps plugs of tobacco)." [204] "In addition to being a stimulant, the beverage also acts as a diuretic, causing increased perspiration. And the Indians sometimes used it as an emetic. On these occasions they would drink it in large quantities, and in a quarter to half an hour they would vomit. Sometimes they would hold their arms across their chests and expell [sic] the contents of their stomachs six or eight feet. The Timucuans would drink repeatedly, vomiting it up each time (fig. 56)." [205]

♠ other ♣ inferred present ♥ Inferred from the following quote from an ethnohistoric account of a similar ritual among the Sioux: "When someone felt that he could no longer endure the smothering effects of the vapor, he might cry out, 'All my relatives,' whereupon the [ritual specialist] would say, 'Open up'." [212] The quote suggests that the accrued vapours in a sweat lodge could lead to a dysphoric feeling of suffocation.

Most frequent collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ daybreak ceremony ♥ "(1) The King or Chief residing on the primary mound in a given Mississippian community is joined by other high ranking nobles every morning before dawn. They face the East and chant as the sun rises, giving it assistance to break free from the grip of the underworld; (2) The King or Chief is carried across the plaza to the mound topped by the charnel structure containing the bones of the dead kings or chiefs after the sunrise. He feeds the fire in front of the charnel house, a fire that is supposed to never go out, and appoints a keeper of the fire to ensure it is fed the remainder of the day. He then goes into the charnel house and awakens the ancestors." [252] These rituals "are really part of one 'daybreak' ceremony I think—they were apparently done every day to ensure the world continued operating properly. Both would have taken place at Cahokia. Probably in each mound group at Cahokia." [253]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ daily ♥ These rituals "are really part of one 'daybreak' ceremony I think—they were apparently done every day to ensure the world continued operating properly." [254]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ daily ♥ These rituals "are really part of one 'daybreak' ceremony I think—they were apparently done every day to ensure the world continued operating properly." [255]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ daily ♥ These rituals "are really part of one 'daybreak' ceremony I think—they were apparently done every day to ensure the world continued operating properly." [256]
♠ Duration ♣ [1-2] ♥ hours. 1-2 hours. Because this ritual focusses around the moment of daybreak [257], it seems reasonable to infer that it did not last more than two hours.
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Expert did not provide estimate.
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ "(1) The King or Chief residing on the primary mound in a given Mississippian community is joined by other high ranking nobles every morning before dawn. They face the East and chant as the sun rises, giving it assistance to break free from the grip of the underworld; (2) The King or Chief is carried across the plaza to the mound topped by the charnel structure containing the bones of the dead kings or chiefs after the sunrise. He feeds the fire in front of the charnel house, a fire that is supposed to never go out, and appoints a keeper of the fire to ensure it is fed the remainder of the day. He then goes into the charnel house and awakens the ancestors." [258]
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ elites ♥ "(1) The King or Chief residing on the primary mound in a given Mississippian community is joined by other high ranking nobles every morning before dawn. ♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ whole polity ♥ Based on the expert's description, this ritual would have been quite public--part of it took place atop monumental mounds, and part of it involved the King crossing Cahokia's plaza [260].

♠ other ♣ ♥

Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ summer/autumn feast ♥ "All of the lines of submound evidence point in the direction of a series of short-term and large-scale events involving feasts, the use of magicoritual and sumptuary goods, the shaping of craft goods and wood, and possibly the rethatching or reconstruction of architecture. The abundant insect remains clearly indicate that these events occurred during warm weather months. Fruits in the pit indicate both early summer (strawberries and mulberries), late summer (blackberries, elderberries, and grapes), and fall (persimmon) harvests, although all could have been eaten from dried stores. The deer and waterfowl may have been most easily captured in the early autumn (see Bent 1962) and certainly abundant starchy-seed crops would have been harvested by that time. Thus, a mid-summer to early autumn origin, at least for zones D2 and G, is most likely." [292] "It is worthy of mention that prominent individuals, such as those buried in Cahokia's Lohmann phase Mound 72 (Fowler 1991, 1997; Fowler et al. 2000), may have played key roles in Cahokia's commensal politics. However, most people taking part in such events would not have been of high rank or of esteemed reputation. So, regardless of who hosted or coordinated the presumed Grand Plaza rites, the events themselves would have been collective experiences that defy pigeonholing into status categories. Such events would have been simultaneously low status and high status or communal and political (see Pauketat 2000; Pauketat and Emerson 1999)." [293] "[T]he sub-Mound 51 pit dates to the earliest phase of the youngest and largest Mississippian polity in North America and may encapsulate the process whereby people accepted or accommodated (or even resisted) a Cahokian organization, identity, or way of life. That process seems to have involved a dramatically enlarged and centralized sense of community, polity, and economy. [...] That coordinated practices may have been components of a collective cultural process may be central to explaining how people accommodated the social and demographic shifts though to have attended the Lohmann phase regime in the Greater Cahokia region (see Pauketat 1998b)." [294]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ "All of the lines of submound evidence point in the direction of a series of short-term and large-scale events involving feasts, the use of magicoritual and sumptuary goods, the shaping of craft goods and wood, and possibly the rethatching or reconstruction of architecture. The abundant insect remains clearly indicate that these events occurred during warm weather months. Fruits in the pit indicate both early summer (strawberries and mulberries), late summer (blackberries, elderberries, and grapes), and fall (persimmon) harvests, although all could have been eaten from dried stores. The deer and waterfowl may have been most easily captured in the early autumn (see Bent 1962) and certainly abundant starchy-seed crops would have been harvested by that time. Thus, a mid-summer to early autumn origin, at least for zones D2 and G, is most likely." [295]

♠ Duration ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ [2,400-20,900] ♥ This estimate is based on the number of ceramic vessels found in a series of discrete feasting contexts at Cahokia--specifically, 20,961 vessels were found in Zone D, 5,501 in Zone D1, 7.782 in Zone D2, 474 in Zone E (which in many ways is an anomalous context, and has therefore been excluded from my estimate), 2,466 in Zone F, 18,685 in Zone G, and 12,615 in Zone H [298]. If we consider that each of these vessels may have been used by a single participant, then the number of vessels should be the equivalent of the number of participants. Of course, each participant may have used a number of different vessels, so perhaps it is more sensible to halve the estimate. Expert guidance needed.

The chunkey game involved opposing teams alternately rolling a finely polished disk-shaped stone across a level play- ing field (courtyard or plaza) and hurling gaming sticks after it to score points." [308]

♠ human sacrifice of an out-group member ♣ inferred absent ♥ Human remains found in association with feasting residue at Cahokia are more likely to have been magicoritual items than the result of human sacrifice performed at the feasts themselves: "Cut marks were observed on a left fibula (both anterior and posterior to the midshaft) and on a right radius (inferior to the tuberosity on the proximal radius) suggestive of postmortem processing. [...] Carnivore gnawing in the form of tooth-grove furrows and tooth punctures was present on theproximal end of a left femur, the proximal end of a right ulna, and a right radius. [...] [T]he polish on a right femur was probably not a natural, postdepositional effect of sand abrasion, and the femur fragment may have been used as a tool. This fact, along with the cut marks and the evidence for the carnivore gnawing, indicates that these particular elements had been retained, used, and exposed for an indeterminate period of tie, perhaps in a mortuary facility such as a charnel house or temple (see DePratter 1991)." [314]

♠ human sacrifice of a relative ♣ inferred absent ♥ Human remains found in association with feasting residue at Cahokia are more likely to have been magicoritual items than the result of human sacrifice performed at the feasts themselves: "Cut marks were observed on a left fibula (both anterior and posterior to the midshaft) and on a right radius (inferior to the tuberosity on the proximal radius) suggestive of postmortem processing. [...] Carnivore gnawing in the form of tooth-grove furrows and tooth punctures was present on theproximal end of a left femur, the proximal end of a right ulna, and a right radius. [...] [T]he polish on a right femur was probably not a natural, postdepositional effect of sand abrasion, and the femur fragment may have been used as a tool. This fact, along with the cut marks and the evidence for the carnivore gnawing, indicates that these particular elements had been retained, used, and exposed for an indeterminate period of tie, perhaps in a mortuary facility such as a charnel house or temple (see DePratter 1991)." [316]
♠ other ♣ ♥

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ warrior purification ritual ♥ Chemical traces of caffeine-rich emetic known as the “Black Drink” in the ethnohistoric literature found on Mississippian beaker vessels dating as far back as 1050-1250 CE [317], some of which are decorated with images of warriors and warfare [318]. According to Crown et al [319], Buchanan [320], and Dye [321], these are sufficient grounds to infer that, like Native American cultures encountered by European colonialists in the Eastern half of North America in later centuries, prehistoric Mississippian warriors purified themselves before and after battles or raids by consuming this substance. Again, based on both ethnographic parallels and the recovery of serving vessels alongside the Black Drink beakers, these purification rituals probably also involved feasting and/or fasting, and they almost certainly strengthened group solidarity (Buchanan 2014, 274). The idea we have of this ritual may be further fleshed out thanks to the following passage summarising ethnohistorical accounts concerning the indigenous peoples of Southeastern North America: "In addition to being a stimulant, the beverage also acts as a diuretic, causing increased perspiration. And the Indians sometimes used it as an emetic. On these occasions they would drink it in large quantities, and in a quarter to half an hour they would vomit. Sometimes they would hold their arms across their chests and expell [sic] the contents of their stomachs six or eight feet. The Timucuans would drink repeatedly, vomiting it up each time (fig. 56). [...] Caffeine stimulates the central nervous system, exciting it at all levels. [...] It enables a person to have a more rapid and clearer flow of thought, makes him capable of more sustained intellectual effort, and sharpens his reaction. It also increases his capacity for muscular work and lessens fatigue. Moreover, some evidence suggests that large doses of caffeine speed up blood clotting. These effects are pronounced with doses of 0.5 to 1.0 grams--the equivalent of three to six cups of strong coffee. When consumed in large quantities, black drink could have delivered at least this much caffeine, and perhaps as much as 3.0 to 4.0 grams. These effects from large quantities of black drink could have been important and even decisive factors in activities such as the ballgame or warfare." [322] In addition to this, Dye [323] writes that "Warfare rituals changed through time, but the basic ritual structure may have remained essentially the same from late prehistory into the Colonial period. Based on the above description, warfare rituals would have included the creation of sacred space; elite involvement and sponsorship of rituals, especially feasting; use of sacred paraphernalia and props such as horns and feathers of supernaturals, plants, crystals, pipes, paints, and consecrated vessels; emphasis on symbolism in food, behavior, and color; consumption of medicines, including the black drink and the war medicine, for ritual cleansing and purification; acquisition, display, and ritual manipulation of war trophies; and ritual performance, including dancing with raptor tails or wings."
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ suspected unknown ♥ It seems likely, based on ethnohistoric accounts [324], that the ritual would have taken place both before and after battles or raids, but it is not very clear how frequent battles were in the Mississippian world. Might be good to ask expert.
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ suspected unknown ♥ It seems likely, based on ethnohistoric accounts [325], that the ritual would have taken place both before and after battles or raids, but it is not very clear how frequent battles were in the Mississippian world. Might be good to ask expert.
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ suspected unknown ♥ It seems likely, based on ethnohistoric accounts [326], that the ritual would have taken place both before and after battles or raids, but it is not very clear how frequent battles were in the Mississippian world. Might be good to ask expert.
♠ Duration ♣ [72-96] ♥ hours. 3-4 days. Similar rituals described in ethnohistoric accounts lasted about 3 days: "This cycle of fasting, feasting, smoking, medicine consumption, vomiting, striking the war post, and dancing continued for three additional days and nights." [327]
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Perhaps this could be calculated from the number of serving vessels usually found alongside Black Drink beakers (which may well have been shared), but such a specific study has not yet been carried out. Might be good to ask expert--perhaps the size of a Mississippian war party could be estimated based on ethnohistoric parallels.
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ substantial minority ♥ warriors. Referring to ethnohistoric accounts of similar rituals, Dye [328] writes that "[p]articipants included the members of the war expedition: scouts, various warrior grades, war priests, doctors, and the war chief and his assistant(s)."
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ fasting ♣ inferred present ♥ According to the ethnohistoric literature, it was tradition for warriors to fast as part of black drink rituals, though feasting was also part of these rituals [329].

♠ fear ♣ inferred present ♥ Inferred from the fact that these rituals were linked with war, and either took place just before battle, or reminded participants of their duty to fight.

"In addition to being a stimulant, the beverage also acts as a diuretic, causing increased perspiration. And the Indians sometimes used it as an emetic. On these occasions they would drink it in large quantities, and in a quarter to half an hour they would vomit. Sometimes they would hold their arms across their chests and expell [sic] the contents of their stomachs six or eight feet. The Timucuans would drink repeatedly, vomiting it up each time (fig. 56)." [334]

Also, not part of Dye's [336] list of the things making up this ceremony's "basic ritual structure [...] from late prehistory into the Colonial period."
♠ risk of death ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the fact that the participants were likely about to go to battle, which suggests that it would have made little sense to risk killing them beforehand. Also, not part of Dye's [337] list of the things making up this ceremony's "basic ritual structure [...] from late prehistory into the Colonial period."

Limits on Power of the Chief Executive[edit]

Legal (formal) limits[edit]

♠ Executive power is separate or independent from military ♣ inferred absent ♥ Formal chiefdoms existed at Cahokia by 1050 CE.[359] "After about A.D. 1100 there is an increase in numbers of palisaded sites (they were present earlier at Toltec)." Additionally, "warfare is widespread after ca. A.D. 1100."[360] Iconography may indicate professional officers or soldiers of some kind. People that look like they are wielding weapons with head scalps attached to belts. Depictions of people who look like they are going to and from battle. Coded inferred absent on the basis that the chief would usually have control over the military specialists of a chiefdom.
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from legislative ♣ inferred absent ♥ Formal chiefdoms existed at Cahokia by 1050 CE.[361] "Members of the highest social strata probably included chiefs, sub-chiefs, elders, priests, and other religious functionaries." [362] "The central administrative complex represents the core of the Cahokian polity. The location of ridgetop mounds within this area may equate with kin groupings or other administrative units."[363] There is no indication that there was a formal legislative body so it is likely that the laws were made by the chief.
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from judiciary ♣ inferred absent ♥ Formal chiefdoms existed at Cahokia by 1050 CE.[364] However, there are no indications of specialist judges or lawyers so it is likely that the chiefs, sub-chiefs and elders enforced the law. "Members of the highest social strata probably included chiefs, sub-chiefs, elders, priests, and other religious functionaries." [365]

Legal (formal) freedoms[edit]

♠ Right to petition executive ♣ inferred absent ♥ There is no indication that there was a government that could have had a formal process to petition the chief. There is no written record for Cahokia[366] so they almost certainly did not have petitions in the sense of a document to present to him. If common people wanted to make a request to the executive one might suppose they made it through an intermediary, such as a lower-ranking member of the ruling class. "Members of the highest social strata probably included chiefs, sub-chiefs, elders, priests, and other religious functionaries."[367]
♠ Critique of executive by government ♣ inferred absent ♥ There is no evidence for a formal government at Cahokia so there was no government to critique the executive. "Members of the highest social strata probably included chiefs, sub-chiefs, elders, priests, and other religious functionaries." [368]
♠ Critique of executive by non-government ♣ inferred present ♥ "Members of the highest social strata probably included chiefs, sub-chiefs, elders, priests, and other religious functionaries." [369] Since there was no formal government bodies to enact his writ we could infer that the chief sought advice, including criticism, from other elites, because he would have needed their cooperation.

Power distributed[edit]

♠ Sanction of executive by government ♣ inferred absent ♥ There is no evidence for a formal government at Cahokia so there was no government to sanction the executive. "Members of the highest social strata probably included chiefs, sub-chiefs, elders, priests, and other religious functionaries." [370]
♠ Sanction of executive by non-government ♣ inferred absent ♥ The executive was the chief. The 'sanctioning' of a chief would be a convoluted mechanism for a low-scale society such as a chiefdom. We could also question whether this would be an explicit 'legal right' given there was no written record[371] that could have been used to define the parameters of the role of 'chief'. If such a process existed whereby a transgressing chief could be fined or sanctioned it most likely would have been customary and/or informal rather than a legal right.
♠ Constraint on executive by government ♣ inferred absent ♥ There is no evidence for a formal government at Cahokia so there was no government to constrain the executive. "Members of the highest social strata probably included chiefs, sub-chiefs, elders, priests, and other religious functionaries." [372]
♠ Constraint on executive by non-government ♣ inferred present ♥ "Members of the highest social strata probably included chiefs, sub-chiefs, elders, priests, and other religious functionaries." [373] Since there were no formal government bodies to enforce compliance we could infer that the chief was constrained in his action by other elites, since his position was dependent on their cooperation.
♠ Impeachment ♣ inferred absent ♥ The idea other elites in the chiefdom could "impeach" the chief seems an over-elaborate mechanic for a society with no written record[374] and no evidence for specialist lawyers.

Customary (informal) checks on executive[edit]

♠ Informal critique of executive ♣ inferred present ♥ "Members of the highest social strata probably included chiefs, sub-chiefs, elders, priests, and other religious functionaries." [375] Since there was no formal government bodies to enact his writ we could infer that the chief sought advice, including criticism, from other elites, because he would have needed their cooperation.
♠ Informal sanction of executive ♣ ♥ The executive was the chief. The 'sanctioning' of a chief would be a convoluted mechanism for a low-scale society such as a chiefdom but obscure, perhaps ritual, punishments for transgressions perhaps cannot be completely ruled out as the chief's position was dependent on the cooperation of other elites. unknown
♠ Informal constraint on executive ♣ inferred present ♥ "Members of the highest social strata probably included chiefs, sub-chiefs, elders, priests, and other religious functionaries." [376] Since there were no formal government bodies to enforce compliance we could infer that the chief was constrained in his action by other elites, since his position, in the absence of formal government bodies, was dependent on their cooperation.
♠ Informal impeachment ♣ ♥ The executive was the chief. The 'impeachment' of a chief would be a convoluted mechanism for a low-scale society such as a chiefdom but obscure, perhaps ritual, impeachment for transgression perhaps cannot be completely ruled out as the chief's position, in the absence of formal government bodies, was dependent on the cooperation of other elites. unknown

Limits on executive power imposed by religious agents[edit]

♠ Critique of executive by religious agents ♣ inferred present ♥ "Cahokia may have been led by a priesthood or a group of ruler-priests, but a shift to 'king' does not appear to have happened at Cahokia."[377] Possibly "specialists in maintaining and performing specific rituals at various community levels."[378] The suggestion that Cahokia could be ruled by a "group of ruler-priests" would strongly imply that decisions made by the leader of the polity required the agreement of other elites; whose opinion, therefore, would likely have been sought in the decision-making process. If Cahokia were ruled by a single priest-chief the situation would be not much different since his position, in the absence of evidence for formal government bodies, could be assumed dependent on the cooperation of other elites.
♠ Sanction of executive by religious agents ♣ ♥ "Cahokia may have been led by a priesthood or a group of ruler-priests, but a shift to 'king' does not appear to have happened at Cahokia."[379] The executive was the chief. The 'sanctioning' of a chief would be a convoluted mechanism for a low-scale society such as a chiefdom but obscure, perhaps ritual, punishments for transgressions perhaps cannot be completely ruled out as the chief's position was dependent on the cooperation of other elites. unknown
♠ Constraint on executive by religious agents ♣ inferred present ♥ "Cahokia may have been led by a priesthood or a group of ruler-priests, but a shift to 'king' does not appear to have happened at Cahokia."[380] Since there were no formal government bodies to enforce compliance we could infer that the chief was constrained in his action by other elites, including other religious agents, since his position, in the absence of formal government bodies, was dependent on their cooperation. This constraint could explain why the "shift to 'king' does not appear to have happened at Cahokia".
♠ Constraint on executive by religious institutions ♣ inferred present ♥ "Cahokia may have been led by a priesthood or a group of ruler-priests, but a shift to 'king' does not appear to have happened at Cahokia."[381] Since there were no formal government bodies to enforce compliance we could infer that the chief was constrained in his action by other elites, including other religious agents, since his position, in the absence of formal government bodies, was dependent on their cooperation. This constraint could explain why the "shift to 'king' does not appear to have happened at Cahokia".
♠ Impeachment by religious agents ♣ ♥ "Cahokia may have been led by a priesthood or a group of ruler-priests, but a shift to 'king' does not appear to have happened at Cahokia."[382] The executive was the chief. The 'impeachment' of a chief would be a convoluted mechanism for a low-scale society such as a chiefdom but obscure, perhaps ritual, impeachment for transgression perhaps cannot be completely ruled out as the chief's position, in the absence of formal government bodies, was dependent on the cooperation of other elites. unknown
♠ Subordination of the chief executive to a religious agent ♣ absent ♥ "Cahokia may have been led by a priesthood or a group of ruler-priests, but a shift to 'king' does not appear to have happened at Cahokia."[383] If the chief or the ruling group were all priests then the position of chief executive was held by religious agents. However, "subordination" to religious agent can occur only when the chief executive was not a religious agent so the code here is absent.
♠ Motivation by belief in supernatural agent ♣ ♥

Mechanisms of Power Transfer (from one head of state to the next)[edit]

♠ Customary mechanism ♣ ♥ "Cahokia may have been led by a priesthood or a group of ruler-priests, but a shift to 'king' does not appear to have happened at Cahokia."[384]
♠ Legal mechanism ♣ absent ♥ "Cahokia may have been led by a priesthood or a group of ruler-priests, but a shift to 'king' does not appear to have happened at Cahokia."[385] Cahokia was a relatively low-scale society that did not have a written record[386] or specialist lawyers. It is unlikely there was a formal legal mechanism to transfer power from chief-priest to chief-priest.
♠ Number of power transitions ♣ ♥
♠ Number of contested transitions ♣ ♥
♠ Number of successful overturns ♣ ♥

Central Bureaucracy[edit]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ inferred absent ♥ There is no written record for Cahokia[387] so there may have been no full-time administrators in a formal state bureauracy. If administrators did exist they may have used a non-written method of record-keeping (e.g. perhaps something similar to knots in string known from early Cuzco) however there is no evidence for this in the archaeological record.

There was, however, what has nevertheless been called a "central administrative complex" and "ridgetop mounds within this area [which] may equate with kin groupings or other administrative units."[388] This might best be understood in the context that "Cahokia may have been led by a priesthood or a group of ruler-priests, but a shift to 'king' does not appear to have happened at Cahokia."[389]

The sites of activity within the "central administrative complex"[390] could have largely been of religious significance and perhaps communal or elite storage rather than used as sites for the administration or processing of taxes and management of records (for which we have no evidence).

♠ Prestige of state-level bureaucracy ♣ ♥

Authority over state-level administrators

♠ Direct ♣ inferred absent ♥ There is no written record for Cahokia[391] so there probably were no full-time administrators in a formal state bureauracy. If administrators did exist they may have used a non-written method of record-keeping (e.g. perhaps something similar to knots in string known from early Cuzco) but there is no evidence for this in the archaeological record. Activity within the "central administrative complex"[392] may have largely been of religious significance and perhaps communal or elite storage rather than used as sites explicitly for the administration or processing of taxes and management of records (for which we have no evidence). One might hypothesise that the new "social roles linked to community defense, organization of labor, and communal storage of maize in secure central places"[393] of the preceding Emergent Mississippian were developed into something more formal and institutional in this period but this does not necessarily mean a state bureaucracy with full-time officials responsible to a king. Whatever institutions were in place at Cahokia to manage resources they did not require a centralised institution for record keeping with an extensive hierarchy of full-time officials.

Whatever institutions were in place at Cahokia to manage resources they did not require a centralised institution for record keeping with an extensive hierarchy of full-time officials.
♠ Land ♣ inferred absent ♥ There is no written record for Cahokia[415] so there probably were no full-time administrators in a formal state bureauracy. If administrators did exist they may have used a non-written method of record-keeping (e.g. perhaps something similar to knots in string known from early Cuzco) but there is no evidence for this in the archaeological record. Activity within the "central administrative complex"[416] may have largely been of religious significance and perhaps communal or elite storage rather than used as sites explicitly for the administration or processing of taxes and management of records (for which we have no evidence). One might hypothesise that the new "social roles linked to community defense, organization of labor, and communal storage of maize in secure central places"[417] of the preceding Emergent Mississippian were developed into something more formal and institutional in this period but this does not necessarily mean a state bureaucracy with full-time officials responsible to a king. Whatever institutions were in place at Cahokia to manage resources they did not require a centralised institution for record keeping with an extensive hierarchy of full-time officials.

Local-level officials (provincial, regional, civic administration)[edit]

♠ Number of regional administrative units ♣ 0 ♥

There were "elite areas" inside the walled district of Cahokia and also "elite areas" outside of it.[427] Cahokia 120 mounds; East St. Louis 45 mounds; St. Louis 26 mounds.[428] "Ridge top mounds may also reflect ritual performances or 'tableaus' associated with these mound and plaza complexes. In this control of ritual activity there may have also have been specialists in maintaining and performing specific rituals at various community levels."[429]

The relationship between the major areas of population outside Cahokia and Cahokia is not clear. They may have been separate polities or ruled by sub-chiefs. They probably weren't explicitly defined regional units lead by 'officials' or governors since Cahokia was a relatively low-scale chiefdom that did not have a central bureaucracy of full-time officials with which to communicate with regional-level officials. Futhermore, scholars believe the "shift to 'king' does not appear to have happened at Cahokia"[430] which may put into doubt the idea that the mound regions with elite areas surrounding Cahokia could have been lead by sub-kings. The dominant view in the literature appears to be that Cahokia was strongly influenced by religious rituals. If the outer regions are not separate polities the relationship between the elite areas was therefore more likely to be a religious one; so perhaps chief-priest to sub-chief priest, characterized by a delegation of ritual responsibilities rather than strictly administrative ones. Scholars do believe "there may have also have been specialists in maintaining and performing specific rituals at various community levels."[431]

Legal System[edit]

♠ Multiple legal systems ♣ ♥ unknown

Formal chiefdoms existed at Cahokia by 1050 CE.[457] However, there are no indications these had specialist judges or lawyers and we have no written record for Cahokia[458] so we don't know if they even had the means to compile records. "Members of the highest social strata probably included chiefs, sub-chiefs, elders, priests, and other religious functionaries"[459] so it is is likely that the chiefs, sub-chiefs and elders enforced a customary law.

Since Cahokia consisted of multiple different groups who may have brought with them and may have still used their own traditions we cannot rule out the possibility there were multiple (customary) legal systems.

"It is clear that different groups came together to create Cahokia, not just one group creating it and others joining it. They came with their prior structure, and remade it at Cahokia. Alignments of differing mound sites may reflect earlier different traditions. People from a number of places came together. This might be part of the problem with understanding Cahokia."[460]

♠ Courts are supported by the state ♣ inferred absent ♥

Formal chiefdoms existed at Cahokia by 1050 CE.[461] However, there are no indications these had specialist judges or lawyers and we have no written record for Cahokia[462] so we don't know if they even had the means to compile records for a centralized bureaucracy to use let alone a court system. "Members of the highest social strata probably included chiefs, sub-chiefs, elders, priests, and other religious functionaries"[463] so it is is likely that the chiefs, sub-chiefs and elders enforced a customary law.

♠ Criminal Prosecution ♣ inferred absent ♥

"Members of the highest social strata probably included chiefs, sub-chiefs, elders, priests, and other religious functionaries"[466] so it is is likely that the chiefs, sub-chiefs and elders enforced a customary law.

Informal Justice[edit]

♠ Non-state judiciary ♣ inferred absent ♥ Formal chiefdoms existed at Cahokia by 1050 CE.[506] However, we have no written record for Cahokia[507] so we don't know if they even had the means to compile records to provide the basis of any type of organization with specific members over-which there could be ajudication.

♠ Religious judiciary ♣ ♥ unknown Formal chiefdoms existed at Cahokia by 1050 CE.[508] However, there are no indications these had specialist judges or lawyers and we have no written record for Cahokia[509] so we don't know if they even had the means to compile records for religious judiciary. "Members of the highest social strata probably included chiefs, sub-chiefs, elders, priests, and other religious functionaries"[510] so it is is likely that the chiefs, sub-chiefs and elders enforced a customary law. To the extent these individuals were defined by their religious roles they could be said to be religious judges.

Property Rights[edit]

♠ Private property (land) ♣ inferred present ♥ "The beginning of the Mississippian period was marked by an abrupt shift in the character of peripheral communities ... The predominantly nucleated pattern of settlement was abandoned in favor of widely scattered single-family farmsteads."[511]

♠ Usufruct ♣ inferred present ♥ "The beginning of the Mississippian period was marked by an abrupt shift in the character of peripheral communities ... The predominantly nucleated pattern of settlement was abandoned in favor of widely scattered single-family farmsteads."[512] The presence of single-family farmsteads suggests that individuals had the personal right to use and enjoy fruits from property.
