Social Mobility[edit]

Status[edit]

Elite status

♠ elite status is hereditary ♣ inferred present ♥ "Cahokia may have been led by a priesthood or a group of ruler-priests".[533] "Ridgetop mounds are virtually unique in the Eastern Woodlands, but there are 16 such mounds at Cahokia. These mounds started out as platform mounds with 'performance' burials ... Their burials created a tableau to legitimize their status and their descendants. They date from ca. A.D. 1050 to 1150, during the peak period of Cahokia."[534] Mound 72 contains elite burials and possible retainer sacrifice.[535] "In Cahokia society, the stockade also must have served a social function. Since the city continued for one mile outside the wall where most of the mounds and extensive habitation areas were located, there must have been some difference between those who lived within the walled district and those who resided outside."[536] (However the palisade around Monk's Mound is thought to have been built "late in the 1100s"[537] which could be the period following this one). The presence of well-defined elite areas, a ruling priesthood and human sacrifice suggest there was low class mobility at Cahokia. Those born within the elite areas probably inherited elite status from their parents.
♠ elite status is selected ♣ inferred absent ♥ "Cahokia may have been led by a priesthood or a group of ruler-priests".[538] Mound 72 contains elite burials and possible retainer sacrifice.[539] "In Cahokia society, the stockade also must have served a social function. Since the city continued for one mile outside the wall where most of the mounds and extensive habitation areas were located, there must have been some difference between those who lived within the walled district and those who resided outside."[540] The presence of well-defined elite areas, a ruling priesthood and human sacrifice suggest there was low class mobility at Cahokia. Those born within the elite areas probably inherited elite status from their parents.
♠ formal system of hereditary social status ♣ ♥ unknown "Cahokia may have been led by a priesthood or a group of ruler-priests".[541] Mound 72 contains elite burials and possible retainer sacrifice.[542] "In Cahokia society, the stockade also must have served a social function. Since the city continued for one mile outside the wall where most of the mounds and extensive habitation areas were located, there must have been some difference between those who lived within the walled district and those who resided outside."[543] The presence of well-defined elite areas, a ruling priesthood and human sacrifice suggest there was low class mobility at Cahokia. Those born within the elite areas probably inherited elite status from their parents.

♠ social mobility ♣ [absent; present] ♥ "Cahokia may have been led by a priesthood or a group of ruler-priests".[544] Mound 72 contains elite burials and possible retainer sacrifice.[545] "In Cahokia society, the stockade also must have served a social function. Since the city continued for one mile outside the wall where most of the mounds and extensive habitation areas were located, there must have been some difference between those who lived within the walled district and those who resided outside."[546] The presence of well-defined elite areas, a ruling priesthood and human sacrifice suggest there was low class mobility at Cahokia. Those born within the elite areas probably inherited elite status from their parents. However, division of labour is evident at Cahokia by 1050 CE[547] so among the non-elites there may have been some possibility to progress to the higher classes relating to occupations, such as handicraft and tool making.

♠ recruitment of chief executive ♣ ♥
♠ extreme status elevation ♣ ♥
♠ extreme status diminution ♣ ♥

♠ occupational mobility ♣ {absent; present} ♥ "Cahokia may have been led by a priesthood or a group of ruler-priests".[548] Mound 72 contains elite burials and possible retainer sacrifice.[549] "In Cahokia society, the stockade also must have served a social function. Since the city continued for one mile outside the wall where most of the mounds and extensive habitation areas were located, there must have been some difference between those who lived within the walled district and those who resided outside."[550] The presence of well-defined elite areas, a ruling priesthood and human sacrifice suggest there was low class mobility at Cahokia. Those born within the elite areas probably inherited elite status - the role of chief priest and/or priest - from their parents. Division of labour is evident at Cahokia by 1050 CE[551] so among the non-elites there may have been some possibility to progress to the higher classes relating to occupations, such as handicraft and tool making. However, the case for occupational specialization is disputed: "There is little evidence for occupational specialization and virtually no evidence for diversification of occupations" [552]; "The case for Mississippian period craft specialization rests on the existence of well-made objects, the great numbers of some particularly attractive items, and the contexts of the tools and debris related to making them."[553]

Slavery[edit]

♠ Proportion of population enslaved ♣ [5-10] ♥ The presence of well-defined elite areas[554], a ruling priesthood[555] and retainer sacrifice[556] suggest there was low class mobility at Cahokia and a large gulf in power between the elite and the non-elite. One might assume due to the presence of retainers that some proportion of the population was enslaved by this elite.

♠ Proportion of population enserfed ♣ 0 ♥ "The beginning of the Mississippian period was marked by an abrupt shift in the character of peripheral communities ... The predominantly nucleated pattern of settlement was abandoned in favor of widely scattered single-family farmsteads."[557] The presence of widely-scattered single-family farmsteads on the periphery might suggest there was readily-available land for individuals to cultivate ("Eric Rupley ... calculated the catchment needed to feed 15,000 people would be 625 square kilometers, which is well within the possible land area available in the American Bottom."[558]) - so peasants would not need to be tied to a landlord. There also is no mention of manor-type houses where one might suppose a powerful landlord would live. The elites live in well-defined "elite areas." The single-family farmsteads do not match the description of a large manor-house or villa. Inside the elite areas the ruler-priests may have had slaves in the form of retainers. There may have been slavery without serfdom at Cahokia if there always was available land to cultivate on the periphery.

One might assume due to the presence of retainers that some proportion of the population was enslaved by this elite.
♠ Elite slavery ♣ inferred absent ♥ The fact that sacrificed individuals from Mound 72 "were biologically distinct to the other buried individuals and were most similar to each other"[562] may suggest that the retainers (if these sacrificed individuals were retainers) were not part of the elite.

However there was no formal state in this period so the corvee labour would likely have been for elite ruler-priests whom were not yet administrators.

Restraints

♠ Owners can execute slaves ♣ inferred present ♥ The presence of well-defined elite areas[566], a ruling priesthood[567] and retainer sacrifice[568] suggest there was low class mobility at Cahokia and a large gulf in power between the elite and the non-elite. One might assume due to the presence of retainers that some proportion of the population was enslaved by this elite. The fact that the elite deemed human sacrifice acceptable suggests they may also have believed they could execute their slaves.
♠ Owners can impose corporal punishment on slaves ♣ inferred present ♥ The presence of well-defined elite areas[569], a ruling priesthood[570] and retainer sacrifice[571] suggest there was low class mobility at Cahokia and a large gulf in power between the elite and the non-elite. One might assume due to the presence of retainers that some proportion of the population was enslaved by this elite. The fact that the elite deemed human sacrifice acceptable suggests they may also have believed they could punish their slaves as they liked.
♠ Corporal punishment by the state ♣ inferred absent ♥ There was no formal state in this period.

Human sacrifice

♠ Prisoners ♣ inferred absent ♥ Evidence for elaborate ritualism at Cahokia by 1050 CE.[572] Mound 72 countains elite burials and possible retainer sacrifice.[573] Strontium analysis on teeth suggests apparent sacrifice victims were not outsiders so presumably they were not prisoners. "Recent studies into the remains of sacrificial victims at the ancient city of Cahokia reveal that those who were killed were not captives taken from outlying regions, as many archaeologists had believed. Instead, they may have been residents of the same community that killed them."[574]
♠ Low social class ♣ inferred present ♥ Mound 72 "found to contain more than 270 bodies — almost all of them young women killed as victims of human sacrifice. ... Dated to between 1000 and 1100 CE, their remains were mostly buried in large pits, laid out in neat rows, and bearing few signs of physical trauma, perhaps killed by strangulation or blood-letting."[575] "Our analysis provide[s] … evidence that suggests the young women may have come from within the region, if not from Cahokia itself."[576] The study found both male and female victims were from within the region, "probably Cahokia itself."[577] Additionally the sacrificed individuals were biologically distinct to the other buried individuals and were most similar to each other.[578] Mound 72 countains elite burials and possible retainer sacrifice.[579]

♠ Retainers ♣ inferred present ♥ Mound 72 "found to contain more than 270 bodies — almost all of them young women killed as victims of human sacrifice. ... Dated to between 1000 and 1100 CE, their remains were mostly buried in large pits, laid out in neat rows, and bearing few signs of physical trauma, perhaps killed by strangulation or blood-letting."[584] Mound 72 "also contained a striking group of outliers: a separate deposit of some 39 men and women, ranging in age from 15 to 45, who — unlike the rest — had been subjected to all manner of physical violence: brutal fractures, shot with stone points still embedded in their bones, even decapitation."[585] The study found both male and female victims were from within the region, "probably Cahokia itself."[586] Additionally the sacrificed individuals were biologically distinct to the other buried individuals and were most similar to each other.[587] Mound 72 countains elite burials and possible retainer sacrifice.[588] Strontium analysis on teeth suggests apparent sacrifice victims were not outsiders so presumably they were not prisoners. Normative Ideological Aspects of Equity and Prosociality[edit]

♠ Ideological reinforcement of equality ♣ inferred absent ♥ "Soon after A.D. 1000 ... a great increase in moundbuilding"[597] may suggest a growing and/or more powerful elite class at Cahokia. Their burials distinguished them from commoners. The following structures could have reinforced equality or inequality - we don't know a lot about them: "Interspersed among the single-family farmsteads were a few structures with special ritual and social significance. They included sweatlodges ... and a large structure that probably had some community significance." [598] Sacrificed individuals in burials shown to be biologically distinct to the other buried individuals and were most similar to each other.[599] Palisade at Cahokia may have had implications for class: "In Cahokia society, the stockade also must have served a social function. Since the city continued for one mile outside the wall where most of the mounds and extensive habitation areas were located, there must have been some difference between those who lived within the walled district and those who resided outside."[600] (However the palisade around Monk's Mound is thought to have been built "late in the 1100s"[601] which could be the period following this one).

Normative Ideological Aspects of Human Sacrifice[edit]

♠ Human sacrifice ♣ [4-5] ♥ At Mound 72, there seems to be a clear distinction between elite burials and those of the sacrificial victims that accompanied them: "The deeper interment, F229-lower, contained a mass grave of an estimated 39 individuals of both sexes who ranged in age from 15 to 45 years (Fowler et al., 1999:70). Several of these individuals displayed skeletal evidence suggestive of violent death, including signs of decapitation, perimortem fractures and embedded projectile points. The bodies of individuals in F229-lower were carelessly positioned in a way that suggested to Fowler et al. (1999:70) that they were executed and then thrown into the open burial pit. These individuals were then covered with several layers of matting that separated this mortuary event from the burial group (F229-upper) interred directly above them. F229-upper contained 15 individuals of both sexes with a diverse age distribution ranging from children to adults. Cedar poles found associated with six of the ten burial units have been interpreted as litters that were used to transport the remains. Varying degrees of articulation and evidence for binding or bundling of remains suggest curation of some bodies prior to burial (Fowler et al., 1999:70-72)." [628]

♠ human sacrifice of an out-group member ♣ {present; absent} ♥ Unknown. Unknown. The following quote says that, of 5 mass burials suggestive of human sacrifice, 4 included victims who had been born and had lived in Cahokia, and 1 included victims that may have been outsiders, but may also have been Cahokians. "Mound 72 is one of the smaller mounds at Cahokia, yet it has received considerable attention. [...] 72Sub2 contained several features, including three mass graves with a sum total of 65 individuals (Features 205, 214, and 237). 72Sub3 contained the largest mass burial (F105), which included remains of an estimated 53 individuals. The demographic profile of primarily adolescent and young adult females (Fowler et al., 1999; Thompson, 2013b) and careful placement of bodies in these four separate mass burial features suggests that the events associated with their construction represent the ritual sacrifice of deliberately selected individuals (Fowler et al., 1999).//During the final phase of Mound 72’s construction, multiple burials were placed outside the limits of the sub-mounds and covered by the final dirt cap. The group most relevant to the present discussion is Feature 229. After erecting the three sub-mounds, a large groundlevel pit was excavated at the southwest margin of the final mound and lined with a fine white sand. This burial feature included two distinct burial units arranged vertically. The deeper interment, F229-lower, contained a mass grave of an estimated 39 individuals of both sexes who ranged in age from 15 to 45 years (Fowler et al., 1999:70). Several of these individuals displayed skeletal evidence suggestive of violent death, including signs of decapitation, perimortem fractures and embedded projectile points. The bodies of individuals in F229-lower were carelessly positioned in a way that suggested to Fowler et al. (1999:70) that they were executed and then thrown into the open burial pit. [...] The elaborate burials and human sacrifices in Mound 72 have been interpreted as clear evidence of marked social stratification and display of power and influence over surrounding communities (Fowler et al., 1999; Porubcan, 2000). The latter interpretation is often influenced by the conclusion reached by Fowler et al. (1999) that individuals buried in Features 105, 205, 214 and 237 were brought to Cahokia as tribute from outside communities. In this study, most individuals analyzed from these features were biologically similar to others in the region and had strontium isotope signatures consistent with being born and living in or near Cahokia. It was also found that individuals buried in F229-lower exhibited a very restricted range of strontium isotope ratios and were dissimilar to others in dental metrics and morphology. This suggests these individuals were derived from a different population than most others buried in Mound 72, and possibly the region as a whole. These findings complement the mortuary context of F229-lower, which was markedly different from any other feature in the mound. Given the available isotopic and biological data, it is unclear whether these individuals resided within Cahokia, or were from a different location with similar geology to Cahokia." [629]
♠ human sacrifice of an in-group member ♣ inferred present ♥ The following quote says that, of 5 mass burials suggestive of human sacrifice, 4 included victims who had been born and had lived in Cahokia, and 1 included victims that may have been outsiders, but may also have been Cahokians. "Mound 72 is one of the smaller mounds at Cahokia, yet it has received considerable attention. [...] 72Sub2 contained several features, including three mass graves with a sum total of 65 individuals (Features 205, 214, and 237). 72Sub3 contained the largest mass burial (F105), which included remains of an estimated 53 individuals. The demographic profile of primarily adolescent and young adult females (Fowler et al., 1999; Thompson, 2013b) and careful placement of bodies in these four separate mass burial features suggests that the events associated with their construction represent the ritual sacrifice of deliberately selected individuals (Fowler et al., 1999).//During the final phase of Mound 72’s construction, multiple burials were placed outside the limits of the sub-mounds and covered by the final dirt cap. The group most relevant to the present discussion is Feature 229. After erecting the three sub-mounds, a large groundlevel pit was excavated at the southwest margin of the final mound and lined with a fine white sand. This burial feature included two distinct burial units arranged vertically. The deeper interment, F229-lower, contained a mass grave of an estimated 39 individuals of both sexes who ranged in age from 15 to 45 years (Fowler et al., 1999:70). Several of these individuals displayed skeletal evidence suggestive of violent death, including signs of decapitation, perimortem fractures and embedded projectile points. The bodies of individuals in F229-lower were carelessly positioned in a way that suggested to Fowler et al. (1999:70) that they were executed and then thrown into the open burial pit. [...] The elaborate burials and human sacrifices in Mound 72 have been interpreted as clear evidence of marked social stratification and display of power and influence over surrounding communities (Fowler et al., 1999; Porubcan, 2000). The latter interpretation is often influenced by the conclusion reached by Fowler et al. (1999) that individuals buried in Features 105, 205, 214 and 237 were brought to Cahokia as tribute from outside communities. In this study, most individuals analyzed from these features were biologically similar to others in the region and had strontium isotope signatures consistent with being born and living in or near Cahokia. It was also found that individuals buried in F229-lower exhibited a very restricted range of strontium isotope ratios and were dissimilar to others in dental metrics and morphology. This suggests these individuals were derived from a different population than most others buried in Mound 72, and possibly the region as a whole. These findings complement the mortuary context of F229-lower, which was markedly different from any other feature in the mound. Given the available isotopic and biological data, it is unclear whether these individuals resided within Cahokia, or were from a different location with similar geology to Cahokia." [630]
♠ human sacrifice of a low-status individual ♣ inferred present ♥ At Mound 72, there seems to be a clear distinction between elite burials and those of the sacrificial victims that accompanied them: "The deeper interment, F229-lower, contained a mass grave of an estimated 39 individuals of both sexes who ranged in age from 15 to 45 years (Fowler et al., 1999:70). Several of these individuals displayed skeletal evidence suggestive of violent death, including signs of decapitation, perimortem fractures and embedded projectile points. The bodies of individuals in F229-lower were carelessly positioned in a way that suggested to Fowler et al. (1999:70) that they were executed and then thrown into the open burial pit. These individuals were then covered with several layers of matting that separated this mortuary event from the burial group (F229-upper) interred directly above them. F229-upper contained 15 individuals of both sexes with a diverse age distribution ranging from children to adults. Cedar poles found associated with six of the ten burial units have been interpreted as litters that were used to transport the remains. Varying degrees of articulation and evidence for binding or bundling of remains suggest curation of some bodies prior to burial (Fowler et al., 1999:70-72)." [631]

♠ human sacrifice of a high status individual ♣ {present; absent} ♥ At Mound 72, there seems to be a clear distinction between elite burials and those of the sacrificial victims that accompanied them: "The deeper interment, F229-lower, contained a mass grave of an estimated 39 individuals of both sexes who ranged in age from 15 to 45 years (Fowler et al., 1999:70). These individuals were then covered with several layers of matting that separated this mortuary event from the burial group (F229-upper) interred directly above them. F229-upper contained 15 individuals of both sexes with a diverse age distribution ranging from children to adults. Cedar poles found associated with six of the ten burial units have been interpreted as litters that were used to transport the remains. Varying degrees of articulation and evidence for binding or bundling of remains suggest curation of some bodies prior to burial (Fowler et al., 1999:70-72)." [633] "To facilitate intramound comparisons, Rose combined individuals from the various burial features into high status, middle status, female sacrifice and litter sacrifice groups using mortuary context, grave goods and location in the mound. The high status group con- tained all individuals from 72Sub1, one feature from 72Sub2 (F219) and F229-upper. The female sacrifice group consisted of the four mass burials comprised of primarily young adult females (Features 105, 205, 214, and 237). The litter sacrifice group consisted of the buri- als in F229-lower. The middle status group included individuals that did not fall into any of the other three categories. Biological relatedness between these groups was assessed using dental morphology." [634] High-status individuals are present, but it is not clear whether they were sacrificed. "At the bottom were the remains of fifty-three sacrificed women, fifty-two of whom were young (most between the ages of fifteen and twenty-five). The fifty-third individual was an “elderly” (thirtyish) female, whom Meyer thought of as “the matron,” sparking the notion that she had been the elder wife of some man’s harem. Since there were no skeletal indications of how the women had died, it is likely that they were poisoned or strangled or that their throats had been slit." [635] "Here, in what would be called Feature 229, Rose and his helpers found an upper layer of fifteen corpses laid into the pit on stretchers, with some bodies doubled up. The cedar carrying poles were still preserved intact on either side of the bodies even nine centuries later. Study of the positions of individual bones on the stretchers led him to believe, like Al Meyer before him, that the corpses of men, women, and children had been tightly bound in burial shrouds, each tied up with multiple cords that terminated in knotted tie beads, again presumably to keep the putrefying bodies together. They were not ordinary sacrifices, Rose reasoned, but possibly important people saved for this special occasion." [636]

♠ Maximum known number of victims in a HS event ♣ 53 ♥ "Mound 72 is one of the smaller mounds at Cahokia, yet it has received considerable attention. Excavations between 1967 and 1971 revealed a complex mortuary assemblage that included at least 25 mortuary features and skeletal remains of an estimated 270 individuals. [...] 72Sub3 contained the largest mass burial (F105), which included remains of an estimated 53 individuals. The demographic profile of primarily adolescent and young adult females (Fowler et al., 1999; Thompson, 2013b) and careful placement of bodies in these four separate mass burial features suggests that the events associated with their construction represent the ritual sacrifice of deliberately selected individuals (Fowler et al., 1999)." [639] Of the other four mass burials found at Mound 72, one included the remains of 39 people, while the other three contained the remains of 65 people between them [640].

Supernatural Enforcement of Morality

For this section, include any type of supernatural punishment or reward either in this lifetime (e.g., curses, good fortune) or in the afterlife (e.g., hell, reincarnation). This includes generalized impersonal punishment/reward (e.g., karma, mana) as well as punishment by specific supernatural beings. Include specific details of the nature and extent of punishment/reward in the description. Code “compound” moral actions (e.g., filial piety = kin + respect) in multiple categories as necessary. Morality categories are based on Moral Foundations Theory (Haidt & Graham, 2007) and Morality-As-Cooperation theory (Curry, 2016).

Human Enforcement of Morality

♠ care/harm ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ fairness/cheating ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ in-group loyalty ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Chemical traces of caffeine-rich emetic known as the “Black Drink” in the ethnohistoric literature found on Mississippian beaker vessels dating as far back as 1050-1250 CE [663], some of which are decorated with images of warriors and warfare [664]. According to Crown et al [665], Buchanan [666], and Dye [667], these are sufficient grounds to infer that, like Native American cultures encountered by European colonialists in the Eastern half of North America in later centuries, prehistoric Mississippian warriors purified themselves before and after battles or raids by consuming this substance. Again, based on ethnohistoric data, as well as the recovery of serving vessels alongside the Black Drink beakers, these purification rituals probably also involved feasting and/or fasting, and they almost certainly bolstered the warrior's courage [668] strengthened group solidarity [669]. However, no evidence of actual enforcement of this value could be found.
♠ respect for authority ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Ridgetop mounds are virtually unique in the Eastern Woodlands, but there are 16 such mounds at Cahokia. These mounds started out as platform mounds with 'performance' burials ... Their burials created a tableau to legitimize their status and their descendants. They date from ca. A.D. 1050 to 1150, during the peak period of Cahokia."[670] However, no evidence of actual enforcement of this value could be found.
♠ purity ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "[B]ased on ethnographic evidence and the presence of “sweat houses” on many Mississippian sites and earlier and later sites), ritual purification was probably the most common [ritual]. This would have involved fasting, sweats, and perhaps taking purgatives (e.g. the “Black Drink”, but also perhaps plugs of tobacco)." [671] However, no evidence of actual enforcement of this value could be found.

Patronage

♠ within-class formal patronage ♣ ♥ Evidence of political alliances, trade and warfare at Cahokia by 1050 CE.[693]
♠ between-class formal patronage ♣ {absent; present} ♥ The presence of full-time craft specialists in the Middle Mississippian tradition (850-1450 CE) is 'hotly debated'.[694] Peregrine writes that if they did exist, they would not have been linked to a 'market system' but 'attached to elite households, producing items for the elites to distribute to the rest of their community'.[695] This might qualify as informal or formal between-class patronage, but I have coded for scholarly disagreement to reflect uncertainty about whether full-time craft specialists existed.

♠ between-class informal patronage ♣ {absent; present} ♥ The presence of full-time craft specialists in the Middle Mississippian tradition (850-1450 CE) is 'hotly debated'.[696] Peregrine writes that if they did exist, they would not have been linked to a 'market system' but 'attached to elite households, producing items for the elites to distribute to the rest of their community'.[697] This might qualify as informal or formal between-class patronage, but I have coded for scholarly disagreement to reflect uncertainty about whether full-time craft specialists existed.

♠ provision of irrigation works ♣ ♥

♠ Health infrastructure ♣ inferred absent ♥ There is no written record for Cahokia[701] so there probably were no full-time administrators in a formal state bureauracy and no health infrastructure. If administrators did exist they may have used a non-written method of record-keeping (e.g. perhaps something similar to knots in string known from early Cuzco) but there is no evidence for this in the archaeological record. Activity within the "central administrative complex"[702] may have largely been of religious significance and perhaps communal or elite storage rather than used as sites explicitly for the administration or processing of taxes and management of records (for which we have no evidence). Whatever institutions were in place at Cahokia to manage resources they did not require a centralised institution for record keeping with an extensive hierarchy of full-time officials. Responsibility of kin group.

♠ Control of infectious disease ♣ inferred absent ♥ There is no written record for Cahokia[703] so there probably were no full-time administrators in a formal state bureauracy and no health infrastructure. If administrators did exist they may have used a non-written method of record-keeping (e.g. perhaps something similar to knots in string known from early Cuzco) but there is no evidence for this in the archaeological record. Entertainment and Knowledge Well-Being[edit]

♠ Funding for festivals ♣ inferred present ♥ Festivals at Cahokia were held inside the main precinct. [715] However these would have been funded by the ruler-priests rather than anything that could be called a formal state institution.

♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ inferred absent ♥ No mention of specialized entertainment buildings in the archaeological literature.

♠ Theatres ♣ inferred absent ♥ No mention of specialized entertainment buildings in the archaeological literature.
♠ Arenas ♣ inferred absent ♥ No mention of specialized entertainment buildings in the archaeological literature.
♠ Ritual buildings/spaces ♣ present ♥ Ritual activity at Woodhenge: "From the center post, the sun priest could observe the rising sun on the eastern horizon as it lined up with certain perimeter posts."[716] "[B]ased on ethnographic evidence and the presence of 'sweat houses' on many Mississippian sites and earlier and later sites), ritual purification was probably the most common. This would have involved fasting, sweats, and perhaps taking purgatives (e.g. the “Black Drink”, but also perhaps plugs of tobacco)." [717]

♠ Full-time entertainment specialists ♣ inferred absent ♥ Unlikely due to lack of archaeological evidence for entertainment buildings or musical instruments and the case for occupational specialization is itself disputed: "There is little evidence for occupational specialization and virtually no evidence for diversification of occupations" [718]; "The case for Mississippian period craft specialization rests on the existence of well-made objects, the great numbers of some particularly attractive items, and the contexts of the tools and debris related to making them."[719]

♠ status of full-time entertainment specialists ♣ ♥

♠ Access to education ♣ inferred absent ♥ There is no written record for Cahokia.[720] "There are no inscriptions, images, or even unambiguous houses or burials of political leaders."[721] This suggests the population was illiterate and there was no education. What education there was may have been oral teachings from elders or priests but without a formal institution.

♠ Life expectancy ♣ 40; 35 ♥ years for men and women respectively. In his encyclopedia entry on the Mississippian tradition from 1100 to 500 BP (i.e. 850 to 1450 CE taking 1950 as 'present'), Peregrine writes: 'Average age-at-death for adult males was about 40 years, and for adult females was about 35 years'.[734]

Economy variables (polity-level)[edit]

State Income[edit]

♠ Tribute ♣ inferred present ♥ There is no written record for Cahokia[746] so there probably were no full-time administrators in a formal state bureauracy to receive taxes or process tribute. If administrators did exist they may have used a non-written method of record-keeping (e.g. perhaps something similar to knots in string known from early Cuzco) but there is no evidence for this in the archaeological record. Activity within the "central administrative complex"[747] may have largely been of religious significance and perhaps communal or elite storage rather than used as sites explicitly for the administration or processing of taxes and management of records (for which we have no evidence). Whatever institutions were in place at Cahokia to manage resources they did not require a centralised institution for record keeping with an extensive hierarchy of full-time officials. Nevertheless there is evidence of political alliances, trade and warfare at Cahokia by 1050 CE[748] due to which we cannot rule out the possibility of tribute payments to the ruler-priests. Scholars have suggested there may have been "fields for the chiefs worked part-time by commoners, or through tribute."[749]

♠ Staple ♣ inferred present ♥ There is no written record for Cahokia[750] so there probably were no full-time administrators in a formal state bureauracy to receive taxes or process tribute. If administrators did exist they may have used a non-written method of record-keeping (e.g. perhaps something similar to knots in string known from early Cuzco) but there is no evidence for this in the archaeological record. Activity within the "central administrative complex"[751] may have largely been of religious significance and perhaps communal or elite storage rather than used as sites explicitly for the administration or processing of taxes and management of records (for which we have no evidence). Whatever institutions were in place at Cahokia to manage resources they did not require a centralised institution for record keeping with an extensive hierarchy of full-time officials. Nevertheless there were "elite areas" inside the walled district of Cahokia and also "elite areas" outside of it.[752] The ruling priesthood[753] likely had retainers.[754] One may ask how this non-productive elite class supported itself if they did not receive wealth through some rudimentary system of taxation?
♠ Labor ♣ [absent; present] ♥ unknown There is no written record for Cahokia[755] so there probably were no full-time administrators in a formal state bureauracy to receive taxes or process tribute. If administrators did exist they may have used a non-written method of record-keeping (e.g. perhaps something similar to knots in string known from early Cuzco) but there is no evidence for this in the archaeological record. Taxation[edit]

♠ Degree of taxation ♣ ♥ unknown There is no written record for Cahokia[767] so there probably were no full-time administrators in a formal state bureauracy to receive taxes or process tribute. If administrators did exist they may have used a non-written method of record-keeping (e.g. perhaps something similar to knots in string known from early Cuzco) but there is no evidence for this in the archaeological record. Activity within the "central administrative complex"[768] may have largely been of religious significance and perhaps communal or elite storage rather than used as sites explicitly for the administration or processing of taxes and management of records (for which we have no evidence). Whatever institutions were in place at Cahokia to manage resources they did not require a centralised institution for record keeping with an extensive hierarchy of full-time officials. Nevertheless there were "elite areas" inside the walled district of Cahokia and also "elite areas" outside of it.[769] The ruling priesthood[770] likely had retainers.[771] One may ask how this non-productive elite class supported itself if they did not receive wealth through some rudimentary system of taxation? "The beginning of the Mississippian period was marked by an abrupt shift in the character of peripheral communities ... The predominantly nucleated pattern of settlement was abandoned in favor of widely scattered single-family farmsteads."[772] Perhaps part of the produce from the single-family farmsteads went to the elite areas. Scholars also have suggested there may have been "fields for the chiefs worked part-time by commoners, or through tribute."[773] Additionally, it is possible that the elite could have supported itself through some sort of tax on trade or pilgrimage. "Exchange networks were well developed and growing probably under the control of high-ranking personages."[774] "It is interesting to note that Cahokia is located not only in an excellent environment for maize production, but also at a geographically central point in the riverine transport network, a point were where tribute might be collected. It might also have made pilgrimage to Cahokia easy for people across the midcontinent."[775] Cahokia "controlled a critical choke point in trade routes that spanned the midcontinent" an idea that goes back to Brackenridge (1813 CE).[776]


Taxes are imposed on:

♠ Land ♣ [absent; present] ♥ There is no written record for Cahokia[777] so there probably were no full-time administrators in a formal state bureauracy to receive taxes or process tribute. If administrators did exist they may have used a non-written method of record-keeping (e.g. perhaps something similar to knots in string known from early Cuzco) but there is no evidence for this in the archaeological record. Activity within the "central administrative complex"[778] may have largely been of religious significance and perhaps communal or elite storage rather than used as sites explicitly for the administration or processing of taxes and management of records (for which we have no evidence). Whatever institutions were in place at Cahokia to manage resources they did not require a centralised institution for record keeping with an extensive hierarchy of full-time officials. Nevertheless there were "elite areas" inside the walled district of Cahokia and also "elite areas" outside of it.[779] The ruling priesthood[780] likely had retainers.[781] One may ask how this non-productive elite class supported itself if they did not receive wealth through some rudimentary system of taxation? "The beginning of the Mississippian period was marked by an abrupt shift in the character of peripheral communities ... The predominantly nucleated pattern of settlement was abandoned in favor of widely scattered single-family farmsteads."[782] Perhaps part of the produce from the single-family farmsteads went to the elite areas, although without a formal government system this would have had to be a very rudimentary form of taxation/tribute.

It might also have made pilgrimage to Cahokia easy for people across the midcontinent."[784] Cahokia "controlled a critical choke point in trade routes that spanned the midcontinent" an idea that goes back to Brackenridge (1813 CE).[785]
